wall0159 said...
Just an additional comment regarding the EROEI of 2.4 (and the article you cited). That analysis allows 140 GWh/year for security and surveillance. Also, it allows 600 GWh/year for the manufacture of everything -- including the concrete and metal frames.

Now solar often uses aluminium frames. Aluminium is *extremely* energy intensive to refine. No one would ever refine aluminium using power from PV -- even now, much aluminium is produced at places with "free" power (eg. Iceland's geothermal, or other sites with hydro) because otherwise the electircity costs are prohibitive. I think that including this in the EROEI is misleading and paints an overly bleak picture.

Having said that, I agree with the theme of your post which is that we won't be fueling a society full of electric cars from PV panels ;-).

1/18/14, 6:28 PM

Grebulocities said...
The amount of data storage capacity, as well as processing power, on Earth is truly enormous and still increasing at an exponential rate. I don't expect this to last longer than the next decade or so (heralding Peak Porn and Peak Cat Pictures) but the sheer volume of existing computer chips and other hardware makes me think that at least rudimentary computer technology could persist for well over a century.

In this day and age, electronic devices are subject to planned obsolescence with an expected lifetime in the 3-5 year range, but I suspect that this is largely because consumers would rather buy a new computer than repair their horribly outdated 4-year-old one. A single failure of a critical component is enough to cause people to discard an otherwise functional electronic device.

In a world where new electronics are prohibitively expensive and no better than the old ones, the incentives will shift toward maintaining computing devices for long periods of time, as well as toward cobbling together homemade computers from the trillions of electronic components that have already been manufactured (sifting through the broken ones to find the ones that work). I suspect that enough working electronic components will be salvageable that basic computing will decline quite slowly, still existing into the 22nd century.

Of course, among the simplest things you can do with a computer is use it to perform arithmetic. Given that electronic calculators appeared in the mass market well before full-fledged computers and that they're simple to make, I think that the calculator will be around for quite a while.

If anything, this makes preserving old ways of doing math even more critical - if the last calculators disappear long after slide rules and abacuses (and maybe even widespread knowledge of pencil-and-paper calculation) have disappeared from living memory, mathematics could easily suffer a much worse decline.

As for what I'm doing personally, I have a slide rule and know basically how to use it (although I'm not very good at it), and I just found an abacus for $3.50 on ebay. I want to learn the abacus well enough to be able to use a "mental abacus" to speed up my mental arithmetic. I also like that they can be used for all four basic functions and are accurate to as many significant figures as you could need, although I suspect slide rules are still better for uses like trig functions, exponents, logarithms, etc.

Moshe, back before the grid reached rural areas, many households had radios anyway, powered by batteries which were usually charged by windpower. You had an A battery and a B battery, one to heat the tube emitters and one to provide operating voltage. It seems to have worked fairly well. At the same time, the Soviets were mass producing thermoelectric generators powered by a single kerosene lamp that kept radios powered all across Siberia, so the absence of grid power clearly isn't an insuperable obstacle.

Enrique, one thing that people on the rightward end of the spectrum tend to lose track of is that ethnic communities tend to be shredded in the course of a civilization's fall, and what comes out of the mess isn't a restoration of old ethnic communities, but the emergence of new ethnicities. Consider the post-Roman history of Britain, in which millennium-old tribal identities went away forever and survivors from all over Roman Britain, hanging onto survival in Wales and Cumbria, redefined themselves as "compatriots" -- that's what Cymry, "Welsh," literally means. A plausible future history of Europe might see southern and central Europe occupied by a völkerwanderung from the Middle East, while the descendants of all of today's European peoples -- concentrated, perhaps, in Scandinavia, the Alpine regions, and the mountainous parts of far western Europe -- redefining themselves as members of the same new national communities, irrespective of where their ancestors came from.

John Michael Greer said...
Mortal, of course not; like everyone else in this business, I make hard choices between what I need to do to function in the existing industrial society (for example, make a cash income) and what I'd like to do in terms of being self-supporting. Still, I'm baffled by your claim about food preservation. Solar drying, root cellaring, pickling, fermentation, and many other methods of food preservation don't require cheap energy, unsustainable agricultural practices, or slavery, and there are plenty of books out there about low-tech methods like these.

Mkroberts, historical examples suggest that there's a lot to be gained by trying to get useful knowledge and skills through any approaching bottleneck; those societies where such an effort takes place generally have an easier time returning to stability and relative prosperity after the dark age than those that don't.

Cherokee, that's a great example of resilience vs. efficiency. Old and clunky beats new and fragile every time once conditions get rough...

Wall1059, you know, this is interesting. For a very long time, people who've wanted to dismiss what I was saying have almost inevitably turned either to cornucopian or to apocalyptic claims. Sunseeker, energy payback time is not EROEI, because it doesn't include a share in the whole system needed to make a given energy technology fit into the broader economy and society. If those systems actually had a 20+:1 EROEI, people who installed them would be making money hand over fist, instead of having to depend on government subsidies and going broke when those get cut.

Croatoan, if that's the technology that appeals to you, by all means get going on it, and share what you learn.

Crow, you can do a certain amount of functional weather forecasting without computers in two ways. The first is to have some basic meteorological instruments, track the weather you get, and learn what tends to follow what in your area. The second, if there are at least a few hundred miles of settled country to the west of you, is to have a radio and find out what weather they're getting today -- that's fairly often what you'll have tomorrow, adjusted for local climate and topography. Beyond that, yes, an umbrella and some tolerance for weather's vagaries are good to have!

Roger, my point is that historically, in the twilight years of big civilizations, the local warlord generally provides better service for less cost than the former imperial bureaucracy did. That's one of the reasons that warband culture so reliably mutates into feudalism, and why feudalism is so resilient and so successful until urbanization comes in. More on this as we proceed!

Joseph, excellent. That's exactly the point -- and the question that follows, then, is how technologies that don't rely on unicorn horn dust can compete with the unicorn industry. The answer? Dave, if coal-to-liquids fuel was that cheap compared to petroleum, energy companies would be piling into CTL like nobody's business. That's not happening -- which shows that your reasoning's flawed. By the way, Allied bombing campaigns didn't succeed in crippling any other part of Germany's wartime economy -- production of new planes for the Luftwaffe, for example, increased steadily right up until the end of the war -- so you're going to have to come up with something better than Allied bombers to explain why CTL didn't keep the Wehrmacht fueled. The reason, of course, is the very low EROEI, which made CTL uneconomical even with vast amounts of slave labor to dig and mine the coal. South Africa's SASOL CTL scheme, which used a similar process, was just as uneconomical in practice -- it had to be propped up by large subsidies, and even so, never really worked well.

Marcello, no argument there. We'll be talking about the prospects for fascism in the deindustrial future in a couple of weeks.

Moshe, no argument there either. Electricity just isn't that economically viable unless you have extravagant amounts of fuel to burn to generate it, and don't have to worry about the losses.

Joseph, the problem with spark-gap is that it isn't limited to a single band -- it sprays radio waves across the spectrum. A slightly more complex design with a crystal for frequency regulation will give you a nice tight signal, which you can then control with a Morse code key. Is it Skype? No, but ham radio operators who are good at CW (Morse code transmission) routinely beat the pants off teenage texters in competitions to see who can get the most information through fastest.

1/19/14, 8:22 AM

Moshe Braner said...
Joseph Nemeth said:

"Low-tech shortwave radio. ... if all you need is to get a message through, why not use a spark-gap radio? This was the first kind of "radio" used for wireless telegraphy. ..."

As JMG already said, spark-gap is very "broadband" in the sense of splattering the power over a broad range of frequencies. That meant that it was useful only in the earliest days of radio, when there were only a handful of radio networks around the world, for use only by the richest entities, e.g., the company that sailed the Titanic.

Narrow-band radio transmission is not too hard, although it does require tubes or transistors. Narrow band reception is a whole 'nuther game, requiring a lot of amplification in multiple stages (each one with a tube or transistor) plus multiple resonant circuits, some of them tunable... There is a reason why hams of my generation often built their own CW transmitter but scrounged for a factory-built receiver.

1/19/14, 9:44 AM

Moshe Braner said...
Re: coal to liquids (CTL). The equipment infrastructure needed is very expensive (in money, materials, and energy). Once you have it in place, it is not terribly expensive to run (as long as the equipment does not wear out, and there are decent coal mines nearby). That is why the CTL refineries set up in South Africa when it was under anti-apartheid sanctions are still in use today, but none have been built in other countries (other than Germany during WW2). There are rumors that China is building some, but it is not clear at what scale, and whether it is anything more than a military venture: to ensure some domestic liquid fuel supply in an emergency, rather than a basis for the economy. Coal-fueled electricity is the basis of their industrial economy, albeit they are literally choking on the fumes.

Some time in 2012, at the same time Mr. Greer was writing about the fall of empires, I read a small blurb in the Wall Street Journal. The article was a middling article buried in a middle page of the B or C section of the paper so not anything alot of people would read or worry about but it carried obvious overtones for anyone willing to see. It was about the preferential treatment the ECB and smaller commercial banks were giving to German industry. Mr. Greer---"Cathy, that BBC piece is stunning. High carbon steel is what you need for really good knives, swords, and many classes of tools. I had no idea that was being made that long ago! It's certainly doable in a low-tech setting -- some of the best steel on Earth, tamagahane steel (the raw material for the finest Japanese swords), is made by hand using pine charcoal in a very simple smelter -- but it's fascinating to learn that the trick was worked out so much earlier than older histories of technology thought.

Mr. Greer, I would be surprised if someone of your extensive background reading in ancient technology had never encountered Wootz steel. The techniques that made it are now lost but the archaeological evidence for it goes back to 300 BCE. While it probably wouldn't be an exaggeration to say that the Japanese were the pre-eminent metal workers of pre-modern history, tamagahane isn't intrinsically a noteworthy steel. Japanese swords acquire their exceptional qualities and characteristics not from the steel but from the exceptional knowledge the Japanese steel workers have of forging technique. Wootz on the other hand is a very good steel. India probably had the pre-eminent metallurgists of the pre-modern period. One of the what-ifs discussed by blade weapon enthusiasts is what might have been if Japanese metal workers had been able to apply their fantastic knowledge of forging technique to that fantastic steel.

Shining Hector said...
Interesting how it seems it's your most fanciful or your most practical posts which generate the most comments.

As far as sustainable healthcare, it's probably both easier and harder than most people expect. Easier because the truly effective things we do aren't horribly resource intensive or complicated, harder because getting there is basically going to require a reboot of the entire system and the slaughtering of numerous sacred cows which will probably end up angering almost every stakeholder. I'll try and keep it brief.

From my perspective, one big problem is that our reach exceeds our grasp. You probably imagine the purpose of having a healthcare system is to treat otherwise healthy individuals who come down with something or other, get the condition treated, and go along their merry way. This does indeed happen, but that doesn't cost a whole lot, because the interventions usually aren't that complicated and once someone gets better, they're better and no longer need us. What really gets expensive is keeping people who are never going to get better "alive" no matter the cost or outcome.

Healthcare is also expensive because of all the regulations that have to be followed and the crazy markups for drugs, medical equipment, etc. A night at the hospital didn't always cost a month's salary, but with all the documentation, staffing requirements, etc. it does. I haven't really come up with a valid thermodynamic reason that a new drug takes literally 4 orders of magnitude more expense to the drug company to make than the bottle of 400 generic Benadryl you can buy at Costco for $3.99. Whenever I open up a pack of light blue Prolene sutures, I like to wonder if they came off the same assembly line as the fishing line they look identical to and were shunted aside, labeled "for medical use", and marked up 4000% like the tins of "premium" meat in Upton Sinclair's The Jungle.

You mentioned the AMA monopoly, but really doctor's salaries are only a small fraction of healthcare expenses. At a rough guesstimate, a typical primary care doc probably throws around 30 times their salary in resources, counting support staff, prescriptions, imaging, referrals, etc. An ICU intensivist probably comes out to more like 100 times or more of their salary. We get paid a lot, but we're responsible for a lot, too. We might be the visible face of the medical establishment, but our golf games are not we're not what most of your hard earned money is going to support. Subject your idyllic alternative care practitioners to the same regulatory and legal environment, and give them responsibility for the same patients and marshaling the same resources as opposed to making the basically healthy feel better about themselves, and they might not look so cheap.

1/19/14, 3:37 PM

August Johnson said...
@Moshe – I think the thousands of Amateurs in the early days of radio would disagree with you about spark being “for use only by the richest entities, e.g., the company that sailed the Titanic.” The ARRL was formed and a tremendous amount of inter-continental and trans-continental communications was done by Amateurs, who weren’t rich, with only spark. I’m not disagreeing with you about it being broad, but there were many, many stations on the air using it.

A couple very good books about the beginnings of Amateur Radio and all that was done with spark:
200 Meters & Down
The World of Ham Radio 1901-1950
Both are available from the ARRL.

Also, narrow band reception isn’t now and wasn’t then reserved for factory-built receivers, just look through the issues of QST and the ARRL Handbook up to even the 1970’s, lots of 2, 3 and 4 tube and transistor regenerative and even superhet receivers to be built from scratch.

My father built several receivers for CW work in the 1930’s by scrounging tubes from the local radio repair shop’s trash pile. The first new tube he bought came at a price, he painted his parents whole house to get the money to buy it. The Hams today build simple regenerative receivers and use them on the air for CW aren’t doing without “narrow-band” receivers. Yes, they’re not up to the performance of the $2000 factory rigs but they are very useful in the crowded band today. The idea that hams had to buy a factory-built receiver to get decent performance is false. Many times all they could afford was to build their own.

Examples
Tube regenerative
http://makearadio.com/tube/tube-radio-schematics.php

Transistor regenerative from 1968
https://www.arrl.org/files/file/Technology/tis/info/pdf/6803024.pdf

Bill Pulliam said...
About "sustainable" law enforcement -- from what I have seen in rural America, having a small, locally-organized community does NOT provide a check on graft and corruption in law enforcement. On the contrary, it seems to promote it. It is far too easy for a corrupt sheriff to control an election by a delicate balance of intimidation and favoritism when the electoral margin need be only a few hundred votes. And many in the electorate want a sheriff who will do them special favors; they don't actually want bind justice and fair-handed rule-of-law. As I say around here, if you want to find the dogfighters, cockfighters, and meth cookers, drive around the countryside in election season and look for the yard signs for the incumbent sheriff. This is by no means an unusual situation; as far as I can tell it is the norm in rural areas.

Any checks that exist on local law enforcement come down from the State and the Feds. The ballot box is less than ineffective at removing, or even limiting, corruption; instead, it rewards it.

Bottom line -- just stay away from the people who are involved in those activities. Make your own circle of friends who are just not connected to the world of local law enforcement or organized crime. If you need the law, and the sheriff doesn't know you very well, you will probably get reasonable response. Just be sure not to give him any means by which he can pressure you into his sphere (i.e. anything that is either a threat, or an opportunity to exert control). Clean (in a legal sense) and modest living are your best defenses against lawlessness; you just don't attract much attention that way, which is a good thing.

And, if someone did break into your place and take some of your stuff, odds are it was your own nephew. Really. Not kidding. Handle it inside the family.

And be REALLY judicious about what beds you jump in to.

I guess the short version -- sustainable law enforcement is about being a peaceful member of your community. You don't attract trouble, and you will get help if trouble finds you anyway. Don't expect much from your sheriff; ideally he will barely know your name.

dltrammel said...
Two additional points:

About skills, there should be noted a definite division. Skills that will be useful in the next Dark Age and will help us get through it.

And "bridging" technologies. Those are skills which need the energy input of cheap oil but will help us as we de-evolve into a more sustainable future.

Solar Panels are a good example. Unaffordable once the subsidy of cheap oil goes away and parts break down yet important to helping us cope.

Solar Thermal tech is one that we will keep and use in the next Dark Age. Things like south facing buildings and thermal syphon window units that can be built by hand will be common then.

---

The second point is that a technology stands a better chance of being retained if the advocator for that tech can make the argument it has dual use, both for public and military.

Future warband leaders are more likely to support lense grinding and glass making efforts is you justify it as a way to provide their military with superior telescopes and binoculars. Not glasses for the masses.

Low tech short wave radio, has obvious military uses too. Letter press printing can be used for military manuals and training.

As much as I hate it, making your case why in a world with limited resources, your tech should be supported is going to probably come down to justifying it to the local ruling elite.

And the military that keeps them in power.

Nastarana said...
Shamans knew, and probably still know, how to read weather signs.

Consider the biblical account of the 12 plagues from a secular perspective. As at least one historian has pointed out, the sequence of (most of the) plagues is what would happen during a record high Nile inundation. That in turn would have been caused by an exceptionally massive monsoon. Moses, tending his flocks and watching the clouds pile up to the south, day after day, would have known what was coming. Pharoah's magicians should also have known, but, then as now, functionaries tend to tell Pharoah whatever he wants to hear.

We tend to forget that the ancient words which get translated 'magician' don't necessarily mean Harry Potter with a wand.

Joseph Nemeth said...
From the Wikipedia page on crystal radios (http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crystal_radio):

"In the early 1920s Russia, devastated by civil war, Oleg Losev was experimenting with applying voltage biases to various kinds of crystals for manufacture of radio detectors. The result was astonishing: with a zincite (zinc oxide) crystal he gained amplification.[34][35][36] This was negative resistance phenomenon, decades before the tunnel diode. After the first experiments, he built regenerative and superheterodyne receivers, and even transmitters. However, this discovery was not supported by authorities and soon forgotten and no device was produced in mass quantity beyond a few examples for research. Crystodyne was produced in primitive conditions; it can be made in a rural forge, unlike vacuum tubes and modern semiconductor devices."

ViewFromHere said...
Dornier re: accumulated silt from dams, etc…—in addition to the phosphorous (beneficial) that would accumulate, we also have a fair amount of mercury from our continued legacy of coal fired power. I’d like to test the ‘duck weed’ that grows on our ponds and sloughs, thick enough you can pick it up from the water in handfuls. That might be a way to harvest the phosphorous without harvesting the mercury. As a soil scientist, I would love to test soils in a 100 mile radius of coal plants and compare with prevailing wind maps. But since I live just 20 miles from one such plant, I’m ‘afraid’ of what I would learn about my own soil. How would my organic farm stand up if we find we have high soil mercury levels?
I’m living in an area that has undergone great demographic lose (50%) since the 1980’s farm crisis. I am bearing witness to the decay of infrastructure. I’m literally watching buildings collapse all around- barns on long vacant farmsteads falling in, grand 3 story brick Main St. buildings collapsing since the roof could not economically be maintained, consolidated school districts leave lovely schools sitting vacant in every small town, lack of remaining people to tend to the basics of community- serving on boards, providing leadership, delivering the Meals on Wheels, 4-H clubs …). In other words, I’m living through and have a front row seat to the Long Descent. And like JMG has said, we are coping by ‘harvesting’ the remaining parts of that ‘glorious past’ to build a different future. The bricks from the lovely 3 story building that collapsed are being used to build a small one story building. We used the equipment from the closed schools to build a community kitchen in a vacant Main St. building so local farmers can process their foodstuffs—like a mini processing plant. So far have made hard apple cider, maple syrup, grape juices and jams, and more in the past few months.
Per JMG’s comment about weather forecasting, we can watch our pastured cattle increased hunger for hay. They seem to know another artic vortex is on the way and have ‘tucked in’ to the hay at a greater rate. I so love having a husband who knows his animals so well that he can read their behavior.
In terms of adapting to a limited future, I start with my everyday life. I mind my ‘wants’ and train myself to be absolutely happy with what I have. I also pass this onto my kids. Let’s celebrate that we are warm on this cold night! Huzzah! Our dinner prayers is “For what we have, let us be truly thankful!” Our family of 5 downsized our house size, refuse to make any house upgrades for aesthetics only reasons, and are careful to invest in things. This way of thinking is a cure for western civ malaise, in my opinion. There are always improvement projects- gardens, compost, to say nothing of the larger farm projects. And I do want to work beauty into our farm.
Of great interest, on a dangerously cold day, you can look at the various plans drawn up by North Dakota State University being in the 1920’s through the 1980’s for barns, cisterns, rammed earth tampers (1925), solar collectors (1970’s), vegetable storage and much more. This is a nice collection of the legacy of a public university that sought to serve the small and mid-sized farmers around them. Check out the Misc. for plans for community buildings (1920’s) that I still see on the landscape around me in the township halls dotting the landscape. http://www.ag.ndsu.edu/extension-aben/buildingplans
Wonderful list of skills to preserve from you JMG and all the readers. One thing I’m seeing lost is animal husbandry. There were 200+ dairy farmers in our county 50 years ago. Now there is one. So many children were once raised knowing much about animals health, reproduction, care, housing, etc… Now that will no longer be passed down. We lose this broad based set of skills at or peril. And the loss occurred in just 2 generations.

Jasmine said...
Dear Mr Greer

Great Blog. I can think of one example in the UK of a transport system which enthusiastic hobbyists and holiday makers helped to preserve though the second half of the 20th century, when it was in danger of being abandoned thanks to increasing use of cars and lorries. The fact that this system has been preserved could help to mitigate some of the affects of declining oil supplies. This is a real world example of how enthusiastic hobbyists can help to keep technologies going,even though main stream society had no need of them at the time because of cheap and plentiful oil.

Throughout the 18th and 19th centuries a vast network of canals was built throughout Britain which helped to facilitate the industrial revolution. The development of the railways in the 1840’s led to a decline in the use of canals. However there was still enough commercial goods being transported on the canal network to keep it goinguntil the second world war. The rise of motorized transport then led to a rapid decline in the use of canals. From an economic point of view the canals should have been abandoned and left to silt up. In the early 1960 there was pressure from governments and local authorities to do exactly this. However Britain is a nation that loves messing about in boats and the canals were increasingly taken over by holiday makers in motorized cruisers and narrow boats. This meant that the canal network remained economically viable and continued to be maintained. Many canal were abandoned around the 1950’s and 60’s. However many local group of hobbyists and enthusiasts went to great effort to bring many of these canals back into use and restored locks and viaducts, because they saw it as a vital part of our heritage. As a result of this country still has an extensive network of canals and has preserved the skills needed to maintain and operate them. There are according to Wiki 2200 miles of canals and navigable rivers in England and Wales. Many of them connected in a network that goes from London to Bristol, Liverppol and many parts of the North and Midlands. This country would also have the skills needed to bring back those canals that were abandoned back into use. Many of these canals were designed for boats that could be pulled by horses so we would have a transport system that could still be used even in the absence of fossil fuels. This network will be vital in the future when it starts to be reused for transporting commercial goods.

daelach said...
@ JMG: The combination of abacus and logarithm tables gives an advantage sliders don't offer: high precision calculations, most notably in concatenated operations. Unless the sliders become really big, their precision is rather limited, especially for elder users.

The abacus serves as a helper for the addition / subtraction of the logarithm values. You could use paper, but paper will be expensive, so the abacus helps to minimise paper consumption. Besides, this can be introduced as steampunk digital computer: The abacus is the CPU plus RAM, the tables are the ROM, additional paper is the harddisk. ;-)

On the other hand, the slider is faster because addition / subtraction are performed using the scales moving relative to each other. So it depends on what precision is really needed.


@ deedl: If the whole base for industrialisation itself fails, then EVERY industry fails with it, and especially one that is so dependent on world-wide distributed raw materials as the computer industry.


@ German military peak oil study, I remember interesting articles in the Australian Defence Forces Journal revealing that they also are aware of this topic:

http://www.adfjournal.adc.edu.au/UserFiles/issues/180%202009%20Nov_Dec.pdf (page 28, Peak Oil Theory)

Plus another one that addresses the complexity of modern military as a problem:
http://www.adfjournal.adc.edu.au/UserFiles/issues/182%202010%20Jul_Aug.pdf (page 44)

The latter one is particularly intriguing because it is a militaryarticle that begins to envision the (military) benefits of LESS. The main point is: bigger, better weapons become useless if the supply chain fails. Simplified: if you don't have ammo, a pistol is less powerful than a spear. Peak oil is explicitely being mentioned in this article, too.

So yes, it's not only the German military that is pondering about peak oil. I bet every major nation's military is. They do know, and so do the governments.

GreenEngineer said...
I'm coming late to the conversation, but I have to raise the question of the EROEI threshold to maintain civilization.

First of all, I you seem to equate "can we sustain industrial civilization" with "can we sustain our current lifestyles". These are two VERY different questions - currently, we're pouring probably the majority of our energy into activities which actively undermine society's continuation. The EROEI threshold for that is obviously going to be much higher than what is required just to keep something resembling industrial civilization.

Note that this can cut two ways: it suggests that we could keep a technological civilization going with lower-grade energy sources; on the other hand, such a civilization is not going to much in the way of luxury, unless those luxuries are restricted to an elite few. So you could have a egalitarian semi-tech culture, but you could equally well (and most likely with current trends) wind up with a high tech feudalism. If you restrict the high-intensity luxuries to 0.1% of the population, you can likely keep going like that for as long as your prole class will put up with it.

Secondly, I want to question your claim that there is any real agreement about a minimum EROEI threshold for technological civilization. Clearly such a limit must exist. By history it's less than about 20:1 and by inspection it must be higher than 1:1. More precisely, it get's hard to say.

I find this post and the associated graph (the second one) very helpful in thinking about this question in particular. As the author points out, at the end of the day, what matters is net surplus energy. The graph in question shows the available surplus energy under a range of EROEI conditions. Two things jump right out:
The difference between a high (100+) EROEI and a moderate one (~15) isn't very much, compared to the difference between a moderate one and a low one (~5). There's a knee in the curve right around 8, after which life gets increasingly more difficult.

In terms of the EROEI of a particular energy source, the difference between EROEI of 12 and EROEI of 8 is very much a function of chosen boundary conditions. But on a system-wide basis, the difference in net surplus energy is quite large. So I don't think we can necessarily conclude that renewable technologies CAN'T sustain a technological civilization - the critical threshold is in the error bands of the various estimates involved. The real answer is a function of the actual EROEI of the energy source, but is also a function of the level of non-energy-producing activities your civilization is trying to sustain, and it's level of energy efficiency.

At least in the context of the built environment (my area of expertise), getting serious about efficiency (I mean, actually serious; not the current level of interest which has a ROI threshold of 2-3 years) could make the difference between crashing at EROEI 12, or sustaining at EROEI 6.

Cherokee Organics said...
Hi JMG,

As a further note regarding the resilience of the electronics required for solar PV in hot weather: The answers came through from the renewable energy community:

- The latest wizz bang battery charger / PV controllers (which perform the super nifty trick of stepping down DC voltages) de-rate approximately 15% to 20% of their upper capacity when ambient temperatures exceed 40 degrees. They’re known as MPPT (maximum power point tracking) and use a funky algorithm (they are not all the same, some are way smarter than others – in this case price is a reliable indicator) to convert volts and amps so as to achieve the highest possible charging voltage and current.

- Those same controllers achieve increased efficiency over older controllers during sunny, cooler weather when the PV panels output higher voltages. The older controllers are PWM (pulse width modulation) which are basically super-fast switching devices.

- The reason for the previous point is that during hot weather, the actual voltage and current generated from the PV panels declines as the surface temperatures of the panels increases. Remember the panels generate electricity with light - not heat. People often confuse this point. Heat actually reduces the energy generated.

The funny thing is that so many people love to think of massive PV arrays in deserts (I suspect because the land is not built on and therefore out of sight). However, this is very sub optimal conditions for PV power generation. Pretty amusing huh? Add in a reflective concentrator and you will actually further reduce output as heat will be even more concentrated on the PV panels.

The final point that they raised was that the controller I use, because it actually does less functions (ie. The newer designs have to do the super-fast switching but also DC to DC voltage step down), it doesn't get as hot. One conclusion that they came to was that in these conditions the PWM controllers were actually having lower losses than the alternative newer controllers. Interesting stuff.

I really respect the people on the renewable energy forum as some of them are years ahead of me in terms of hands on experience in all sorts of areas.

As to the winter performance of solar PV, all I can add is that my winters are a lot sunnier than Germany. Honestly, I sometimes feel like telling people, "install it and then we'll discuss how it works in the real world."

PS: Is this week a frack load of comments or what? (I always feel a bit naughty using the word frack).

John Roth said...
Daelach:

A lot of people miss the distinction between things that are counted and things that are measured. Things that are counted (coins, apples, oranges, etc.) can use an abacus, things that are measured (length, weight, etc.) can use a slide rule. They need fundamentally different mathematics at the practical level.

Unknown said...
(Deborah Bender)

@Clarence--Thanks for the link to the foot-powered clothes washer. On the plus side, it's portable, the spin dry feature is good and might prove useful for other household tasks, and so is leaving the operator's hands free.

On the minus side, because it's made of plastic, nothing besides the handles and seat cushion can be manufactured, repaired or replaced with local tools and materials.

I also have doubts about the ergonomics. Since the operator has to sit on top of it to press the pedal, she or he has to have legs of the right length in order for the necessary repetitive motion to be comfortable. I didn't check to see whether pedal height adjusts.

$40 would be a whole lot of money for an Andean villager. If there's no way to repair the thing when parts are damaged or worn, it's not such a good investment, even if she pays off part of the cost by taking in washing. Just another expensive imported gadget destined for the midden.

The design you end up with depends on the criteria you set at the start. If portability was the priority so women can wash clothes at the water source rather than carrying water home for washing, it makes a great deal of sense to use plastic.

Otherwise, separating the agitation and spin dry into separate devices would make them easier to make with locally sourced materials, tools and skills. I've got armchair notions for how both could be built.

cromberg said...
JMG,

You state that your academic background is in the history of ideas. Have you ever wondered how humans got the idea that they are fundamentally different from other species on this planet, when the facts are that human bodies do not include any unique chemistry or structures, and they do not exhibit any genetic behavioral imperatives other than to survive and reproduce, just like all other species?

Have you wondered why humans believe they are a special case that is not bound by the evolutionary rules that govern all other species, and that this exempts them from evolution’s penalties?

Have you wondered why humans believe that they exist on two separate planes, one physical and the other spiritual, and that this bestows the ability to choose their behavior on a moral basis that overrules their baser animal instincts?

Our speech and logical reasoning resides in our left brain. External stimulus that reaches our right brain, and our older structures, the hypothalamus and amygdala, does not transfer to our left brain as speech. Instead, it is transferred as genetically derived emotion, which the left brain often interprets as spiritual. These emotions disengage rational thought whenever our genes perceive a threat to themselves or to their alleles, without our left brain even being aware of it.

All this is fleshed out in great detail, with many referenced studies, in “The Spirit in the Gene,” by Reg Morrison, which I highly recommend. There is an excellent summary of Morrison’s main points here:
http://regmorrison.edublogs.org/files/2012/06/gamblers-3-2fe1qvj.pdf

Here is one of Morrison’s concluding statements, which may not make complete sense until you read the link referenced above:

“The widespread acceptance of such credos as creationism, astrology and sustainable economic growth gives adequate warning that the fraction of the population capable of applying even the most basic rules of evidence to mystically derived information is so small as to be inconsequential. It means also that the most seductive and dangerous forms of mysticism, those that underpin racism and religious fundamentalism, are totally bullet proof.
By selectively preserving the mystics among our hominid ancestors evolution not only gave us the weapon that would catapult us from obsolescence to world domination, it seems also to have taken out a shrewd insurance against our overwhelming success. Only such a deliciously rewarding and tamper-proof device as mysticism could have prevented us from foreseeing the danger of overpopulation a long, long time ago.”

I suspect you can't make a living espousing Morrison's ideas (amply demonstrated by the fact that Morrison's book is out of print), as there is great resistance to the idea that we are genetically driven just like any other animal. So I understand that you must continue to emphasize near term preparation for the long descent. I just thought you would be interested.

The issues of corruption and selective enforcement mostly arise in the realms of organized crime. In my area at present, this takes the form of cock fighting, dog fighting, other gambling activities, a small amount of prostitution, and a lot of illegal drugs. Worth noting that these drugs are banned by the feds, not the local laws. I can't imagine in the absence of the federal ban that marijuana prohibition would survive a month here. It is only the threat of federal charges that keeps the pot growers and distributors under the thumb of the sheriff's office.

So you do mostly have a choice. If you don't engage in any of the fighting, gambling or prostitution, and you limit your recreational chemistry to what you can grow yourself, you can for the most part stay out of the way of the graft.

For the record, I vote in every local election. When I don't know the names on the ballot, I talk to trusted friends who have been here longer. Another thing about rural politics, the names on the ballot are not strangers. They are people you see around town and do business with; some will be folks you know quite well. Eventually you may even find yourself listed there.

Gunnar Rundgren said...
I think the development of renewable energy in Sweden is a good case for what could have been done in some countries such as the US if the transformation of the energy system had started early enough. The share of renewables in Sweden went from 25% to 50% from 1970 until today. 1970 most renewable energy was hydroelectricity which was already exploited fully at that time (the 4 non exploited rivers shall remain so according to parliament decision long rime ago). Added to 68 TWh of hydro there is 8 TWh wind and 140 TWh of biomass, mainly in the form of wood. Solar is hardly measurable, Most of this is with old technologies and not all those hyped break-through technologies. Of course, I still with your assessment that it will not be possible on a global scale to run industrial consumer society for 10 billion people on renewables. Sweden is sparsely populated and is blessed with water and forest (not much sun though...) and in addition, it has, like most rich countries, "outsourced" a lot of its manufacturing to other countries.

Matt Mc said...
GreenEngineer, there's some interesting analysis here on the EROEI thresholds on how much is needed to sustain civilisation:

A brief guide and a packet of seeds

"The system identifies two critical thresholds. The lower of the two is the “viability threshold”, at an EROEI of about 6:1. At this point, energy costs absorb over 14% of economic output, making the economy barely viable at all.

"Well before that point is reached, however, there is the “investment threshold”, set at an EROEI of 14:1. At this point, the cost of energy is 6.7%. Whilst the remaining 93% of output may suffice to keep the economy going, it is insufficient to fund net investment in new capital assets."

The author Tim Morgan authored many of the Tullet Prebpn analyst reports over the last couple of years.

1/21/14, 2:45 PM

John Michael Greer said...
Cromberg, when anybody starts rattling off canned talking points starting with "Have you ever wondered..." they lose my interest very quickly. Yes, I wondered, then I did some reading and study. One of the things I learn from the history of ideas is that the sort of hardcore behavioral determinism you're pushing is a very popular belief system when people aren't comfortable with their own actions, usually because of a mismatch between those actions and their official values. It's always comforting to be able to blame your genes, or divine providence, or some other outside force for your own choices -- and it's not too hard to make a deterministic claim into a closed-loop argument that's immune from disproof. Equally, when people want a good excuse to blame other people for their actions, free will ideologies are popular, because then you can insist at the top of your lungs that it's their fault.

Step outside the world of self-justifying ideologies and it's tolerably clear that human beings, like all other living things, are partly constrained and partly free, and that it's possible to increase the scope of individual freedom to some extent by bringing biological and cultural automatisms into consciousness and subjecting them to the "strange loops" of reflective awareness. Of course that middle view doesn't support anybody's agenda of blame, so it tends to get neglected -- even though it's the only one that escapes self-contradiction.

Juhana, there again, you're not paying attention to the lessons of history. The ruling classes are among the very first to go up against the wall when a civilization tips over into decline, and once fossil fuels become a major energy sink, those warlords who figure out how best to do without them will be much more likely to survive and triumph over the ones that cling to them.

Nano, the military's been on this for something like a decade. It's just the civilian leadership that's clueless.

Gunnar, exactly. We could have done so much if we'd have kept going with the promising starts made in the 1970s! As it is, that's water over the dam, and what matters now is what can be done with the limited time we've got left.

Hugo, glad to hear it. I hope it does some good!

sunseekernv said...
@nuku - what it says to me is that Property-Assessed Clean Energy (PACE) has come to New Zealand.

(And gee, with 72% of New Zealand electricity from hydro and geothermal, no wonder PV in NZ has a way to go. But you guys got Tokelau 100% solar powered - cool!)

The intent is for cities/counties (property tax collecting authorities) to help get more energy efficiency and/or renewables out there. Since many people (now) move every few years, many homeowners don't want to pay for efficiency/weatherization/renewables as a lump sum up front, because they figure they'll be moving before they get payback. And such things are still considered outré, so they fear they won't get their money back if they sell before payback.

Or they just lack the capital, either in truth or because the new SUV/mondo flat screen TV/designer kitchen got a higher priority.

So the local governments use their highly rated debt to get cheap money, and lend it to homeowners to do the upgrades/installs, then the homeowner pays the government agency back over typically 10 - 20 years.

When one buys electricity from the power company, they do the financing of the power plant under the covers, and the ratepayers indirectly pay back the utility company's creditors over time. But if an individual buys their own mini power plant (or a bunch of insulation/good windows/etc.), the individual must come up with the capital explicitly. (or do PACE where it's offered, or lease a system from SunRun/Sungevity/Solar City/SunPower/...)

How many people buy cars or houses or make big "home improvements" with cash?
Most people take out a loan. PACE is just a loan from a foresighted local government.

In the U.S. and other places withOUT decent Feed In Tariffs and/or decent economics (like Hawaii, but now HELCO is freaking that so many people are going solar) - banks are skittish about lending to mere mortals doing "weird stuff".
In places like Germany, with a stable FIT and stable/fair policies (and lots of experience), and now grid parity, banks will loan for PV systems no problem. No need for PACE there.

old, but basic info on PACE
http://www.nrel.gov/docs/fy10osti/47097.pdf

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PACE_financing

DSIRE (Database of State Incentives for Renewables and Efficiency) claims to be in flux, but still up.
http://www.dsireusa.org/
One can search for Incentive/Policy = PACE
but there's only a few listed.

GreenWizard note - those of you considering insulation/windows/upgraded HVAC/solar hot water/... might want to look thru DSIRE - may as well snag some gov't money/tax breaks/... as long as they're available. Maybe any of you who are business owners or worker bees thereto could look into this if relevant.

This seems to be the clearing house for PACE:
http://pacenow.org/
Oh - I see.
I thought the Fannie Mae/Freddie Mac ruckus was resolved logically - but I guess not. Residential PACE programs (on any federally guaranteed loans - i.e. most in the US) may be nixed by the "helpful" bureaucratic control freaks of the Federal Housing Finance Agency.
Well, I guess I'm glad commercial PACE programs are still going on unfazed, the more energy efficiency/renewables out there, the softer the descent will likely be.

Moshe Braner said...
Re: sextant navigation. The sextant may be low-tech, but for full navigation (not just latitude) you need a "chronograph", i.e., an accurate clock. That was the high-tech invention that basically launched the British Empire. These days one can get OK accuracy from a $5 digital watch, perhaps we'll have those for a while yet. But this reminds me of a joke: low-tech navigation instructor: so you use the sextant like this, and you take the reading and the exact time and you look it up in this book... Student: and where do I get the exact time? Instructor: easy, you read it off your GPS receiver...

Emmanuel Goldstein said...
Hi all,
Whatever else you do, try to get a copy of this book;
Kachadorian, James. The Passive Solar House: Using Solar Design to Heat and Cool Your Home. White River Junction, Vermont; Chelsea Green Publishing, 1997. www.chelseagreen.com, ISBN: 0-930031-97-0.

This is a book of designs and building principles that allow you to make a home entirely heated by passive means; Sunlight in Winter, and radiant and heat exchange cooling in Summer. No exotic building materials are used.

The book was written by a contractor who built and sold these homes for 15 to 20 years, starting in Vermont. I am definitely going to use these floor plans and principles to make my final house.

Key features include South (ie., equatorial) orientation, salt-box design, and a passive heat storage design that the author calls a "solar slab."

1/22/14, 9:01 PM

GreenEngineer said...
JMG,

Just to be clear, I am well aware of the inverse relationship between efficiency and resilience, and I know just how hard it is to say "well, we can always get more efficient". Energy efficiency is what I do for a living, more or less.

That said, in an economy as ornate as ours, there are plenty of things we do which make us both less efficient and less resilient. Glass box skyscrapers, for example, or our addiction to SUVs.

And that's precisely my point. I have no illusions that we can keep our current lifestyles down at EROEI of 10:1. There is a lot of waste in the system, but not enough to squeeze us down that far and still give us glass skyscrapers, SUVs and cheap air travel.
However, I'm pretty sure we could keep (more or less) our current standard of living - which is distinct from lifestyle - at those low EROEIs if we can be at all smart about our resource allocation (the probability of that does seem pretty low, but see below.) For example, personal automobile ownership is probably not possible at 8:1, but a high degree of personal mobility (at least local mobility) probably is. (Or we can keep 1% of the population in SUVs while everyone else walks, but that circumstance is probably not stable for more than a generation.)

Responding to Matt Mc: That is a very interesting link and I will have to study it at length. But in principle I agree with the assertion that there is a threshold of viability which is pretty low, and a (much higher) threshold of investment. Or, to put it another way, there's a threshold of viability for technological civilization, and another threshold of viability for modern capitalism (i.e. the investment threshold).
As we cross the higher threshold, the economic system will show increasing signs of stress. At some point, the only thing which will keep the system going in our inability to believe in alternatives. (It may be that we're at this point already.) In this view, our future as a technological species depends on how we react in the space between the investment threshold and the viability threshold. If we let the old system die, then maybe we can figure out a new approach. Or we can ride the old system right on down, past the real threshold of viability - at which point, technological civilization is in fact utterly doomed.

Based on my personal experience, this is really key. This breakdown of the old order will, hopefully, take with it a lot of our modern expectations and illusions. And that is key, from the point of view of technical sustainability. I can design a building - even a large, high occupancy commercial building - to remain comfortable in all kinds of weather, with very little energy use.
I cannot design such a building to operate effectively without the active participation of the occupants. Occupant behavior is central to the effectiveness of radically green buildings. Currently we expect the building to keep us comfortable without any mindfulness or involvement on our part, and THAT is the truly insurmountable design barrier. If during the breakdown we are able to let go of those expectations, a whole new vista of potential low-energy solutions will open up for us.

1/23/14, 5:51 PM

AlanfromBigEasy said...
My analysis is much along the same lines as GreenEngineer, with some additional thoughts.

A mix of renewables, with EROEI > 100 to EROEI 50 hydro mixed in with lower EROEI solar, wind and biomass, can have an acceptable net EROEI even if some components are low. The weighted average is what is important.

Quite a few hydropower installations will never silt up (almost 5 GW at Niagara Falls for example). And even those that do skit up can be easily reconfigured to run-of-the-River hydropower schemes.

WAY too much of our current GDP in the USA is consumption (not true of China). Diverting several percent to energy conserving and producing investment can have a significant cumulative impact.

Fund insulation tax credits instead of invasions, buy solar panels instead of a Caribbean vacation, etc etc. Same GDP and energy use, but MUCH better long term results.

I have little doubt, unlike you, that viable long term mitigations (NOT solutions !) exist.

My pessimism is based on the unlikeliness of our collective self making logical decisions in our own long term self interest.

1/24/14, 1:31 PM

Bogatyr said...
@dltrammel - Sorry for the late response, but anyway - no apology needed! I can quite understand, having done more than my share of 12-hour days in the recent past.

I sympathise as well: I used to be be involved in politics, and I sometimes found it incredibly frustrating that people just didn't seem to understand that there were really important things that needed doing to address social problems etc... Of course, other people had their own, usually more pressing, issues to be dealing with.

Perhaps you've done this, but I suspect you need to reconsider your marketing strategy. 'Marketing' is often taken to be a dirty word, but it isn't: it simply means identifying what people want, and responding to it.

In the case of the audience here at TAR, some readers are interested in the topics JMG covers, but aren't doing much about it in their own lives, or don't know where to start. They don't need the Green Wizards site - or at least, it wouldn't appear to be the most obvious destination.

Some are already deeply engaged and knowledgable, but their time is occupied actually getting things done; contributing to the Green Wizards site would actually consume time that could be used more effectively on their own efforts.

Others, like me, may have identified the skills they want to acquire, and in so doing have also identified the people we need to learn from IRL. As I mentioned before, the Green Wizards site is not useful as a knowledge source but neither are we knowledgable enough to contribute.

A handful have both the time and the knowledge to contribute meaningfully.

So what could you do?

Well, one way is comparatively cheap: identify bloggers who are learning relevant skills, or experts in these fields, and incorporate their RSS feeds into the site. This will take some setting up and curating, but is ultimately easier than having everything written freshly. There are lots of people in traditional crafts etc who have blogs of some kind or other, and people are more likely to write for themselves and people they know on their own blog than to write for strangers on another site.

Secondly, get yourself mentioned, or write guest posts, on related blogs that have a bigger audience. Our host has mentioned several times, I think, that his readership has been boosted after TAR was mentioned elsewhere, bringing in curious new readers.

You may also want to think about the look and feel of the GW site. At the moment, it's very bare-bones, which may work for TAR, but a lot of people will be more comfortable with photos and an attractive design. Having just visited it to take a look, you also need to sort out the hosting - the URL still contains "http://teresamcguffey.com/", so some settings are wrong somewhere.

Anyway, just a few thoughts that are meant to be helpful!

1/25/14, 11:59 PM

nwlorax said...
Dear Fellow Readers:

I don't respond often because well and truly, the Peak Oil Debate shaped all of my childhood and youth through college and reliving it is usually a no win subject.

I inhaled almost every issue of the AAPG (American Association of Petroleum Geologists) journal, from 1969-1984, edited one article of some import, and three important biomarker species of nanoplankton that differentiate between terrigenous and pelagic sedimentation are named after my sisters. I remember 1972 very clearly, because AAPG statisticians in the "7 Sisters" proved conclusively that many if not most of the small inner city children of the US will freeze to death if lead is removed from gasoline due to the need to divert heating oil into gasoline production. Obviously, this didn't happen. So, let me suggest a useful strategy for evaluating nonsense,or at least improbability applicable across many fields.

An enormous contribution to mathematics made c. 1935 was Benford's law. (Yes, someone else nailed it c. 1890, but Benford published.) Simply stated, it is possible to differentiate between nonsense and useful data sometimes-in some contexts-by looking at the digits. I mention this because so long as counting and differentiating things into groups is a useful skill, probability and statistics will be valuable.

Benford wrote before Eniac et al existed. In fact, an argument could be made that without the physical book that Benford looked at, noting which pages were more discoloured from use than others, his discovery could not have been so easily made.

Had Benford's work been applied to the (fill in the blank) financial crisis, said crisis would have been nipped in the bud by forensic accounting. At least I hope so.

It is possible to do very complex math without digital electronics. Until the early 1960's tide computers in the US (for all coastal areas) were mechanical. That's right, they used rooms filled with gears, offset cycloids and the like, and got the right answers which were then published. The electronic replacements for the tide computers copied the input and derived equations to make a virtual copy of this machine.

Seems to me that using a voice input system to ask a central database a simple question about adding two single digit numbers is not a triumph of technology, but of stupidity. A smarter machine system would respond at a much lower level of complexity and energy use by solving the problem directly or requiring the user to solve it. Back in the 1950's this was known as "Polymorphic Computing" and fell out of favor when memory became less of a constraining factor.

The right questions to unleash computational prowess on might be something like this--

What is lowest erg-consuming device or methodology that can perform operation/calculation X? (No cheating by introducing a brilliant mathematician who survives only on stuff grown on some offshore island.)

We should be looking at ways to design and implement such machines, recognizing that some processing is best done by humans. I can think of one failed Mars mission where the failure to maintain the Metric System in measurements cost ~$670 million dollars.

Some natural cycles are so long that we as a species haven't been keeping records long enough to notice these things. Given that modern Corporate Society is based on ignoring all information older than 90 days, seems to me there is some room for improvement in designing sustainable methods.


1/27/14, 9:03 PM

averagejoe said...
John,
I’ve recently read your book the Ecotechnic Future and I found it fascinating. I enjoyed this post but I would like to add something. As you point out in your book, before we get to the ecotechnic future we will have to go through a scavenging phase, and this will involve reusing and recycling what we can, and this phase interests me, as its likely to directly affect me and my children.

I am a classic car enthusiast, Volkswagens in the main. What I like about old cars, is what I dislike about new cars, and that is their simplicity. An old VW is made of simple components, that doesn’t involve computers and printed circuit boards. It can be repaired using simple tools, and minimal technical knowledge or skill. It also has the ability to be patched up using what is available. I think moving forward, ‘products’ that are like this will be far more resilient in a scavenging phase. More modern cars may be more fuel efficient, but need computers and trained technicians to tell you what’s wrong with them when their computer components fail, which they frequently do. They demonstrate all the failings of “excessive complexity”. Older more simple technology will fair better in my opinion, than the latest technology. I can picture old cars providing good service until they are 100 years old with regular maintenance, whereas modern cars will simply be discarded. Cuba probably is the best example of how simple technology can be repaired indefinitely, patching up using whatever is around, to keep something going by owners and friends with a minimal skill set and tools.

“Excessive complexity” seems to me to sum up all that is wrong with modern day technology. It means to me that it is less resilient. Anything that has mechanical components instead of computer controlled ones, and simple electronics seems more desirable to me. There are some exceptions. I think PV panels will work. But they don’t have any moving components to wear out so they are relatively simple and have a long life span. The inverter will be the weak point however.

So over the next 50 years or so we may witness more use of what are considered to be now ‘old’ technologies resurfacing, because they are capable of being maintained by the average joe, in a world where simplicity, and resilience is key.

Regards
K

About This Archive

This archive contains the posts and comments from John Michael Greer's blog, The Archdruid Report, from May 2006 to June 2017.

Follow Greer's current writing on his new blog, www.ecosophia.net, or on his Dreamwidth account, ecosophia.dreamwidth.org.

The scripts used to produce this archive are hosted on GitHub. A downloadable copy of the archive is available on Google Drive.

Contact the archive creator at squirrel2038 at gmail.com.

About JMG

John Michael Greer is Past Grand Archdruid of the Ancient Order of Druids in America , current head of the Druidical Order of the Golden Dawn, and the author of more than thirty books on a wide range of subjects, including peak oil and the future of industrial society. He lives in Cumberland, MD, an old red brick mill town in the north central Appalachians, with his wife Sara.
